        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075"><meta property="og:title" content="Nanoporous Red Phosphorus on Reduced Graphene Oxide as Superior Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075/asset/images/acsnano.8b04075.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="As a potential alternative to lithium-ion batteries, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted more and more attention due to the lower cost of sodium than lithium. Red phosphorus (RP) is an especially promising anode for SIBs with the highest theoretical capacity of 2596 mAh g–1, which faces the challenges of large volume change and low conductivity. Herein, we develop a nanoporous RP on reduced graphene oxide (NPRP@RGO) as a high-performance anode for SIBs through boiling. Its nanoporous structure could accommodate the volume change and minimize the ion diffusion length, and the high electronic conductive network built on RGO sheets facilitates the fast electron and ion transportation. As a result, NPRP@RGO exhibits a superhigh capacity (1249.7 mAh gcomposite–1 after 150 cycles at 173.26 mA gcomposite–1), superior rate capability (656.9 mAh gcomposite–1 at 3465.28 mA gcomposite–1), and ultralong cycle life at 5.12 A gRP–1 for RP-based electrodes (775.3 mAh gRP–1 after 1500 cycles). The successful synthesis of NPRP@RGO marks a significant enhanced performance for RP-based SIB anodes, providing a scalable synthesis route for nanoporous structures."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075/asset/images/acsnano.8b04075.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/nwyw~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="90f6eeaf283d775c-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6eeaf283d775c-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: 'ab5fedc6-848b-4219-9ef2-7d1721c91e8d'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6eeaf283d775c-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'nano';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/nano/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/nano/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

$(function() {
    var serialCode = "ancac3";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Nanoporous Red Phosphorus on Reduced Graphene Oxide as Superior Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.8b04075", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Nanoporous Red Phosphorus on Reduced Graphene Oxide as Superior Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.8b04075", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Nanoporous Red Phosphorus on Reduced Graphene Oxide as Superior Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.8b04075", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Nanoporous Red Phosphorus on Reduced Graphene Oxide as Superior Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.8b04075", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Nanoporous Red Phosphorus on Reduced Graphene Oxide as Superior Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.8b04075", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Nanoporous Red Phosphorus on Reduced Graphene Oxide as Superior Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.8b04075", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Nanoporous Red Phosphorus on Reduced Graphene Oxide as Superior Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.8b04075", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Nanoporous Red Phosphorus on Reduced Graphene Oxide as Superior Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.8b04075", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Nanoporous Red Phosphorus on Reduced Graphene Oxide as Superior Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.8b04075", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

hlFld-Affiliation"><div class="loa-info--wrapper"><div class="loa-info-name">Lijie Ci</div><div class="loa-info-affiliations"><div class="loa-info-affiliations-info">Key Laboratory for Liquid−Solid class="loa-info-scifinder"><span>More by <a href="/action/doSearch?field1=Contrib&amp;text1=Lijie++Ci" data-id="article_author_search">Lijie Ci</a></span></div><div class="loa-info-orcid"><img height="20" width="27" src="/products/achs/releasedAssets/images/orchid-2856f829046fbda55b90e1582edf0e9a.png" alt="Orcid"><a href="http://orcid.org/0000-0002-1759-105X" title="Orcid class="articleHeaderDropzone4" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone4"><div class="articleRightsPermissionsLink"><a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D7380%26pageCount%3D8%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DShuai%2BLiu%252C%2BHui%2BXu%252C%2BXiufang%2BBian%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D12%26issueNum%3D7%26contentID%3Dacsnano.8b04075%26title%3DNanoporous%2BRed%2BPhosphorus%2Bon%2BReduced%2BGraphene%2BOxide%2Bas%2BSuperior%2BAnode%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D8%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1936-0851%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dancac3%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D7387%26publicationDate%3DJuly%2B2018" data-id="article_rights_permissions">Request reuse permissions</a></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_header-metrics-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="article_header-metrics-dropzone"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075/asset/images/medium/nn-2018-04075r_0006.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075/asset/images/large/nn-2018-04075r_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.8b04075&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">As a potential alternative to lithium-ion batteries, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted more and more attention due to the lower cost of sodium than lithium. Red phosphorus (RP) is an especially promising anode for SIBs with the highest theoretical capacity of 2596 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, which faces the challenges of large volume change and low conductivity. Herein, we develop a nanoporous RP on reduced graphene oxide (NPRP@RGO) as a high-performance anode for SIBs through boiling. Its nanoporous structure could accommodate the volume change and minimize the ion diffusion length, and the high electronic conductive network built on RGO sheets facilitates the fast electron and ion transportation. As a result, NPRP@RGO exhibits a superhigh capacity (1249.7 mAh g<sub>composite</sub><sup>–1</sup> after 150 cycles at 173.26 mA g<sub>composite</sub><sup>–1</sup>), superior rate capability (656.9 mAh g<sub>composite</sub><sup>–1</sup> at 3465.28 mA g<sub>composite</sub><sup>–1</sup>), and ultralong cycle life at 5.12 A g<sub>RP</sub><sup>–1</sup> for RP-based electrodes (775.3 mAh g<sub>RP</sub><sup>–1</sup> after 1500 cycles). The successful synthesis of NPRP@RGO marks a significant enhanced performance for RP-based SIB anodes, providing a scalable synthesis route for nanoporous structures.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D7380%26pageCount%3D8%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DShuai%2BLiu%252C%2BHui%2BXu%252C%2BXiufang%2BBian%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D12%26issueNum%3D7%26contentID%3Dacsnano.8b04075%26title%3DNanoporous%2BRed%2BPhosphorus%2Bon%2BReduced%2BGraphene%2BOxide%2Bas%2BSuperior%2BAnode%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D8%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1936-0851%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dancac3%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D7387%26publicationDate%3DJuly%2B2018" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2018 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291517&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Batteries" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Batteries" class="article__tags__link">Batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291331&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Composites" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Composites" class="article__tags__link">Composites</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290689&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Oxides" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Oxides" class="article__tags__link">Oxides</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292522&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Two dimensional materials" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Two%20dimensional%20materials" class="article__tags__link">Two dimensional materials</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Nanoporous&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_nanoporous">nanoporous</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Red+Phosphorus&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_red%20phosphorus">red phosphorus</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Reduced+Graphene+Oxide&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_reduced%20graphene%20oxide">reduced graphene oxide</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Anode&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_anode">anode</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Battery&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20battery">sodium-ion battery</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="NLM_p first">Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) with high energy density have been the key power supply for portable electronic devices and widely applied in electric vehicles. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 1 to 3">(1−3)</a> But the large-scale application of LIBs in smart grids is limited by the high cost and insufficient lithium resources. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4 ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 4 and 5">(4,5)</a> Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are the most promising alternatives for LIBs in large-scale energy storage applications, due to the lower cost and more abundant sodium resources. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6 ref7'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7" aria-label="reference 6 and 7">(6,7)</a> Moreover, the chemical and physical properties of sodium are similar to those of lithium. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8 ref9 ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref9 ref10" aria-label="reference 8 to 10">(8−10)</a> However, it is impossible to simply adopt the current strategies of LIBs to design high-performance SIBs due to the larger radius of the sodium ion (0.097 nm) than that of the lithium ion (0.068 nm). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9 ref11 ref12'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref11 ref12" aria-label="reference 9, 11 and 12">(9,11,12)</a> Reported cathode materials for SIBs have exhibited a capability comparable to their lithium-ion counterparts, and the main challenge for competitive SIBs is to design high-performance anodes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16" aria-label="reference 13 to 16">(13−16)</a> Graphite as the most widespread anode for LIBs shows a very low sodium storage capacity (30–35 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) due to the crystalline mismatch between the graphite lattice and sodium ions. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17 ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref18" aria-label="reference 17 and 18">(17,18)</a> Among the promising SIB anode materials, such as alloy-based materials, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9 ref19 ref20 ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref19 ref20 ref21" aria-label="reference 9 and 19 to 21">(9,19−21)</a> metal oxides, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22 ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref23" aria-label="reference 22 and 23">(22,23)</a> and nongraphitized carbon, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24 ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25" aria-label="reference 24 and 25">(24,25)</a> red phosphorus (RP), with a high theoretical specific capacity (about 2600 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>), is a great potential anode for SIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8" aria-label="reference 8">(8)</a> However, the RP anode for SIBs shows an exceptional capacity fading due to its low conductivity (about 10<sup>–14</sup> S cm<sup>–1</sup>) and large volume expansion (about 300%) during repeated charge–discharge processes, which may cause large polarizations, severe pulverization of active material, poor electrical contact between RP and the conductive network, and unstable solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) films. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3 ref26 ref27 ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref26 ref27 ref28" aria-label="reference 3 and 26 to 28">(3,26−28)</a> To conquer these impediments, RP-C composites, such as chemically bonded RP/graphene hybrids prepared <i>via</i> ball milling, RP-carbon nanotubes with chemical bonding and a cross-linked polymer binder, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3 ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref29" aria-label="reference 3 and 29">(3,29)</a> RP-multiwall carbon nanotube composites, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> RP-single-walled carbon nanotube composites, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> P@CMK-3 synthesized <i>via</i> a vaporization–condensation process, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10" aria-label="reference 10">(10)</a> and C@P/GA prepared by vapor redistribution, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> have greatly improved the electrochemical performance of RP anodes for SIBs. However, the rate capability and cycle life of RP-based anodes for SIBs is rather poor compared with those of LIBs. The electrochemical performance of RP/carbon-based materials as anodes for SIBs is impeded by the following factors: (1) limited carbon coverage on RP and poor electrical contact due to the simple mixing of carbon and RP; (2) large volumetric change of RP during repeated sodiation–desodiation processes; (3) limited carbon loading level due to the uncontrollability of carbon layer thickness on the RP surface. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> Nanoporous structures have been considered as an ideal structural model that not only can accommodate the volume changes but also benefit efficient ion diffusion <i>via</i> the nanoporous channels, which can avoid pulverization during repeated sodiation–desodiation processes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36" aria-label="reference 9 and 33 to 36">(9,33−36)</a> Moreover, graphene can improve the conductivity of nanomaterials greatly and avoid nanoparticle aggregation effectively, improving the electrochemical performance of electrodes based on it. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> Hence, the nanoporous red phosphorus on reduced graphene oxide (NPRP@RGO) electrode design is considered to be an effective way to solve the aforementioned problems. However, synthesis of the red phosphorus nanomaterial <i>via</i> a wet-chemical method is always a great challenge, not to mention the nanoporous red phosphorus (NPRP). Zhang <i>et</i><i>al</i>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> reported a sublimation-induced synthesis of phosphorus-based composite nanosheets by a chemistry-based solvothermal reaction. Chang <i>et</i><i>al</i>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> reported the large-scale synthesis of red phosphorus nanoparticles (RPNPs) by reacting PI<sub>3</sub> with ethylene glycol. As anodes for LIBs, the RPNP electrodes exhibit a high specific capacity, long cycling life, and excellent rate capability. Moreover, Zhou <i>et</i><i>al</i>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> developed a wet solvothermal method to fabricate hollow P nanospheres with porous shells <i>via</i> a gas-bubble-directed formation mechanism, which needs gas bubble generation during the chemical reaction. Developing an easier method to prepare the NPRP is strongly needed.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Herein, we prepare NPRP@RGO <i>via</i> a typical redox reaction and boiling process. NPRP@RGO shows a high specific capacity (based on RP) of 1848.67 mAh g<sub>RP</sub><sup>–1</sup> at 256.3 mA g<sub>RP</sub><sup>–1</sup> after 150 cycles, excellent rate performance (971.7 mAh g<sub>RP</sub><sup>–1</sup> at 5.12 A g<sub>RP</sub><sup>–1</sup>), and good cycling performance (775.3 mAh g<sub>RP</sub><sup>–1</sup> after 1500 cycles at 5.12 A g<sub>RP</sub><sup>–1</sup>) for RP-based SIB anodes. The advanced electrochemical energy storage performance of NPRP@RGO can be attributed to the nanoporous structures and the high electronic conductive network built on RGO sheets, which not only facilitate ion and electron migration but also can buffer the volume expansion efficiently.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i3" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i3"> Preparation Schematics of NPRP@RGO</h3><div class="NLM_p">Phosphorus triiodide (PI<sub>3</sub>) can be reduced by ethylene glycol with cetyltrimethylammonium bromide (CTAB) to produce red phosphorus. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> In this reaction, the reducing agent is ethylene glycol, and the phosphorus source is PI<sub>3</sub>. The surfactant we used is CTAB, which can inhibit the growth of phosphorus in the reaction solution. Beforehand, a solution of PI<sub>3</sub> in iodobenzene (1.6 M), a solution of graphene oxide (GO) (10 mg/mL), and CTAB (0.018 M) in ethylene glycol (as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075/suppl_file/nn8b04075_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a> 0–10 s) should be prepared. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075/suppl_file/nn8b04075_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a> (16 s), the PI<sub>3</sub> iodobenzene solution is injected into the GO and CTAB ethylene glycol solution with intense magnetic stirring. The color of the solution turns brown after 9 s (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075/suppl_file/nn8b04075_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a> (25 s)), demonstrating the formation of RP. After the PI<sub>3</sub> solution injection, the color of the solution turns from brown to black gradually (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075/suppl_file/nn8b04075_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>, 40–180 s), indicating the prepared RP mixing with GO uniformly in the solution (as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a> (i)).</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075/asset/images/medium/nn-2018-04075r_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075/asset/images/large/nn-2018-04075r_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Preparation schematics of NPRP@RGO. (i) Prepared RP mixing with GO uniformly in the solution. (ii) RP@GO is reduced to RP@RGO by N<sub>2</sub>H<sub>4</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O. (iii) RP@RGO is immersed in residual solution after centrifugation. (iv) Heating in a vacuum drying chamber at 200 °C, the RP is assembled around gas bubbles. (v) With the bubbles growing, more and more disordered RP distributes on the surface of the gas bubbles. (vi) NPRP@RGO is produced after gas bubbles detach.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075/asset/images/large/nn-2018-04075r_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.8b04075&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Then the RP@GO is reduced to RP@RGO by N<sub>2</sub>H<sub>4</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a> (ii)). After centrifugation, the RP@RGO is immersed in residual solution (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a> (iii)). As indicated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a> (iv), then it is heated in the vacuum drying chamber at 200 °C, the solution mainly composed by ethylene glycol and iodobenzene is boiled, and the RP is assembled around gas bubbles. As the bubbles grow, more and more disordered RP distributes on the surface of the gas bubbles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a> (v)). Finally, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a> (vi), with a simple and scalable boiling method, we successfully produce NPRP@RGO after gas bubbles detach. In order to analyze the formation mechanism of the NPRP@RGO, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075/suppl_file/nn8b04075_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2a</a>, a TEM image of the prepared RP@RGO after centrifugation shows that the RP is immersed in residual solution on RGO, corresponding to <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>(iii). After heating in a vacuum drying chamber at 200 °C for 10 min, the amount of residual solution decreases because of the boiling and a small number of nanopores form, as shown in TEM images of the RP@RGO (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075/suppl_file/nn8b04075_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>b–d). With the boiling time increasing, more and more residual solution is evaporated, forming more and more nanopores. As indicated in TEM images (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075/suppl_file/nn8b04075_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>e,f) of the RP@RGO after heating for 1 h, more nanopores form in the RP on RGO compared with that heated for 10 min. Corresponding to <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a> (vi), the residual solution is evaporated completely and the NPRP forms on the RGO (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>f–i and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075/suppl_file/nn8b04075_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2g,h</a>) after heating in a vacuum drying chamber at 200 °C for 6 h. In order to further prove the NPRP@RGO formation mechanism (as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>), the obtained RP@RGO was collected by centrifugation after the reaction. Then it was dispersed in ethanol and stirred to remove the residual ethylene glycol and byproducts. Then the RP@RGO was collected by centrifugation without boiling. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075/suppl_file/nn8b04075_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>, there is no nanoporous structure in the RP particles, indicating that the nanoporous structure is induced by boiling.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075/asset/images/medium/nn-2018-04075r_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075/asset/images/large/nn-2018-04075r_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a–c) SEM images of NPRP@RGO, EDS mapping of elemental P (d) and C (e) corresponding to (b); (f–i) TEM images of NPRP@RGO; (j) STEM of NPRP@RGO and corresponding EDS element mapping images of P (k) and C (l) elements.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075/asset/images/large/nn-2018-04075r_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.8b04075&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4"> Morphology and Structure Characterization of NPRP@RGO</h3><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a–c show the SEM images of the as-prepared NPRP@RGO. The RGO we used is reduced from GO (as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075/suppl_file/nn8b04075_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>). The SEM images (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a–c) and EDS element mapping (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d,e) indicate that NPRP particles are distributed on RGO sheets uniformly and densely. Less than 0.1 wt % iodine was found in NPRP@RGO by energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) analysis. Interestingly, as shown in the TEM images of NPRP@RGO (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>f–i and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075/suppl_file/nn8b04075_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>g,h), the NPRP (with sizes mainly located at 20–100 nm) is distributed on RGO sheets. The scanning transmission electron microscopy (STEM) (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>j) of NPRP@RGO and corresponding EDS element mapping images of P (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>k) and C (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>l) elements further demonstrate the NPRP particles are distributed on RGO sheets. The SEAD pattern of NPRP@RGO (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075/suppl_file/nn8b04075_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2i</a>) includes two sharp polycrystalline rings decorated with multiple bright hexagons, demonstrating an overlay of crystalline RGO nanosheets in the film. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41 ref42 ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41 ref42 ref43" aria-label="reference 41 to 43">(41−43)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a, the X-ray diffraction (XRD) pattern of NPRP shows three broadened diffraction peaks at 12–20°, 25–40°, and 45–70° corresponding to that of commercial RP. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8" aria-label="reference 8">(8)</a> NPRP@RGO exhibits four broadened characteristic peaks at 12.7–19°, 20–27°, 28–42°, and 46–70°, consistent with the characteristic peaks of NPRP and RGO. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b shows Raman spectra of commercial RP, NPRP, RGO, and NPRP@RGO composites. The Raman spectrum obtained for NPRP is similar to that of commercial RP with the presence of characteristic peaks between 300 and 550 cm<sup>–1</sup>. The RGO pattern indicates the characteristic G band (1592 cm<sup>–1</sup>) and D band (1322 cm<sup>–1</sup>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> The characteristic peaks of NPRP@RGO composites combine that of NPRP and RGO clearly. The chemical bonding between RP and RGO sheets in NPRP@RGO composites were investigated with X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS), as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c,d. The P 2p spectrum in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c can be fitted to four Gaussian–Lorentzian peaks at 134.1, 133.2, 129.9, and 129.2 eV, corresponding to P–O, P–C, 2p<sub>3/2</sub> P–P, and 2p<sub>1/2</sub> P–P bonds, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11 ref39 ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref39 ref45" aria-label="reference 11, 39 and 45">(11,39,45)</a> It is worthy to note that the P–C chemical bonding forms between NPRP and RGO, and it can facilitate the intimate and robust conductive contact between NPRP and RGO sheets. The C 1s spectrum in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d can be fitted to five peaks: the O–C═O bond at 289 eV, the C═O bond at 286.6 eV, the C–O bond at 285.3 eV, the C═C/C–C bond at 284.7 eV, and the sp<sup>2</sup> C–P bond at 283.3 eV according to previous literature. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11 ref46 ref47 ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref46 ref47 ref48" aria-label="reference 11 and 46 to 48">(11,46−48)</a> It further proves the formation of C–P chemical bonding between RGO and NPRP. The Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) measurements of NPRP@RGO (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075/suppl_file/nn8b04075_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>) were conducted to obtain the surface area information, and the NPRP@RGO composites have a large surface area of 1192.5 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, while the pore size distribution curves (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e) indicate that there are many micropores and mesopores in the NPRP@RGO composites. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>f exhibits the thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) of the RGO and NPRP@RGO in a nitrogen atmosphere. The as-prepared NPRP@RGO has an obvious weight loss at around 400 °C due to the vaporization of NPRP, and the content of NPRP is 67.6 wt %. Moreover, the RP content in NPRP@RGO can be increased by decreasing the graphene oxide content in ethylene glycol solution under the same conditions (as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075/suppl_file/nn8b04075_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>). The successful synthesis of NPRP@RGO composites may offer robust electrode integrity, fast ion and electron transport, and excellent electrochemical energy storage properties as anodes for SIBs.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075/asset/images/medium/nn-2018-04075r_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075/asset/images/large/nn-2018-04075r_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) XRD patterns of commercial RP, NPRP, RGO, and NPRP@RGO. (b) Raman spectra of commercial RP, NPRP, RGO, and NPRP@RGO. High-resolution XPS spectra of P 2p (c) and C 1s (d) for NPRP@RGO. (e) Pore size distribution curves of NPRP@RGO obtained by HK and BJH methods. (f) TGA of the RGO and NPRP@RGO in a nitrogen atmosphere.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075/asset/images/large/nn-2018-04075r_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.8b04075&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> Sodium Storage Performance Charaterization</h3><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a shows the cyclic voltammogram (CV) of the initial four cycles for the NPRP@RGO at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> between 0.01 and 2.0 V (<i>vs</i> Na<sup>+</sup>/Na). There are two peaks in the first cathodic scan. The peak at around 0.86 V due to formation of a SEI film on the electrode surface <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3 ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref49" aria-label="reference 3 and 49">(3,49)</a> disappears in the subsequent scans, indicating that the SEI formation occurs mainly during the first cycle. Moreover, the sharp cathodic peak between 0.45 and 0.01 V appears due to sodium-ion insertion into NPRP@RGO to form Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>P compounds. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8" aria-label="reference 8">(8)</a> The peak at around 0.71 V during the first anodic scan is related to the desodiation process. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11 ref50'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref50" aria-label="reference 11 and 50">(11,50)</a> In the following cycles, the area of the oxidation peak is almost equal to that of the reduction peak without obvious peak position shifting, implying excellent reversibility of NPRP@RGO as SIB anode. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b displays galvanostatic charge/discharge curves of the NPRP@RGO electrode at a current density of 256.3 mA g<sub>RP</sub><sup>–1</sup> (173.26 mA g<sub>composite</sub><sup>–1</sup>) with a voltage range of 0.01–2 V. The discharge profile consists of three regions located at 0.9–0.38, 0.38–0.24, and 0.24 V <i>vs</i> Na<sup>+</sup>/Na (two sloping regions and one plateau, respectively), while in the charge process three slopes are located at around 0.38, 0.53, and 1.8 V and one plateau at around 0.7 V, consistent with the CV results in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a. Additionally, the capacity–voltage curves of both desodiation and sodiation are well reproducible from 1 to 150 cycles, implying the stable nanoporous structure of the NPRP@RGO and its outstanding electrochemical reversibility during sodiation–desodiation processes. It shows the first charge specific capacity of 2109.5 mAh g<sub>RP</sub><sup>–1</sup> with an initial Columbic efficiency of 78.5%. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c exhibits the cyclic performance of NPRP@RGO and commercial RP electrodes at 256.3 mA g<sub>RP</sub><sup>–1</sup> (173.26 mA g<sub>composite</sub><sup>–1</sup>) within the voltage range of 0.01–2 V. The NPRP@RGO anodes reveal an excellent cycling stability, maintaining a reversible discharge capacity of 1848.67 mAh g<sub>RP</sub><sup>–1</sup> (1249.7 mAh g<sub>composite</sub><sup>–1</sup>, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075/suppl_file/nn8b04075_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>) after 150 cycles. Additionally, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075/suppl_file/nn8b04075_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>, the RGO we used as anode for SIBs, exhibiting a capacity of 58.9 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 173.26 mA/g. Hence, the capacity of RGO in the NPRP@RGO can be neglected. The Coulombic efficiency of NPRP@RGO approaches 99.9% after capacity loss of the initial cycles, implying its excellent reversibility, while the commercial RP anodes exhibit a poor cyclic performance (as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c, RP). In addition to the high capacity and cyclic performance of NPRP@RGO electrodes, they also show a high rate capability, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d. The rate performance of NPRP@RGO anodes is investigated at various current densities from 0.256 to 5.12 A g<sub>RP</sub><sup>–1</sup>. With current density increasing, specific capacities of 2080.2, 1876.5, 1682, 1398.7, and 971.7 mAh g<sub>RP</sub><sup>–1</sup> are obtained at 0.256, 0.512, 1.28, 2.56, and 5.12 A g<sub>RP</sub><sup>–1</sup> for NPRP@RGO, respectively. After 50 cycles of rate testing, when the rate is restored to 2.56 and 0.512 A g<sub>RP</sub><sup>–1</sup>, the specific capacities of NPRP@RGO return to 1350.2 and 1869.2 mAh g<sub>RP</sub><sup>–1</sup>, respectively, demonstrating that the sodiation–desodiation processes of NPRP@RGO anodes are reversible even at a high current density. To further study the superior capacity output and cycling stability of NPRP@RGO as anode for SIBs, it was also evaluated at 5.12 A g<sub>RP</sub><sup>–1</sup> (3465.28 mA g<sub>composite</sub><sup>–1</sup>) (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>e). The capacity–voltage curves (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075/suppl_file/nn8b04075_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>) of both desodiation and sodiation are well reproducible, implying its outstanding electrochemical reversibility during high-rate charge–discharge processes. The NPRP@RGO anodes retain a high reversible capacity of 775.3 mAh g<sub>RP</sub><sup>–1</sup> (524.1 mAh g<sub>composite</sub><sup>–1</sup>) after 1500 cycles at 5.12 A g<sub>RP</sub><sup>–1</sup> (3465.28 mA g<sub>composite</sub><sup>–1</sup>, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075/suppl_file/nn8b04075_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>). <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075/suppl_file/nn8b04075_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a> shows the competitive electrochemical performance of NPRP@RGO compared with that of reported SIB anodes in the literature (only partial reported results over 200 cycles are listed). It is worth mentioning that the NPRP@RGO as anode for SIBs delivers an excellent cycling performance among these results and RP-based electrodes at a high current density of 5.12 A g<sub>RP</sub><sup>–1</sup>. The NPRP@RGO anodes (NPRP@RGO:Super P:carboxymethyl cellulose = 8:1:1 (in weight)) for SIBs also exhibit a high capacity of 505.6 mAh g<sub>composite</sub><sup>–1</sup> at 3465.28 mA g<sub>composite</sub><sup>–1</sup> after 500 cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075/suppl_file/nn8b04075_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12</a>). However, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075/suppl_file/nn8b04075_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13</a>, the RP@RGO (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075/suppl_file/nn8b04075_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>) without the boiling process as anode for SIBs exhibits a poor cyclic performance. It maintains only 87.8 mAh g<sub>composite</sub><sup>–1</sup> at 3465.28 mA g<sub>composite</sub><sup>–1</sup> after 150 cycles.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075/asset/images/medium/nn-2018-04075r_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075/asset/images/large/nn-2018-04075r_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) CV of the initial four cycles for the NPRP@RGO at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> between 0.01 and 2.0 V (<i>vs</i> Na<sup>+</sup>/Na). (b) Galvanostatic charge/discharge curves of the NPRP@RGO electrode at 256.3 mA/g<sub>RP</sub> (173.26 mA/g<sub>composite</sub>) with a voltage range of 0.01–2 V. (c) Cyclic performance of NPRP@RGO and commercial RP electrodes at 256.3 mA/g<sub>RP</sub> within the voltage range of 0.01–2 V. (d) Rate capability of NPRP@RGO electrodes from 0.256 to 5.12 A/g<sub>RP</sub>. (e) Long-term cycling performance of NPRP@RGO electrodes at 5.12 A/g<sub>RP</sub> (3465.28 mA/g<sub>composite</sub>) between 0.01 and 2.0 V. Current density and specific capacity were calculated based on the mass of NPRP.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075/asset/images/large/nn-2018-04075r_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.8b04075&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The excellent electrochemical performance of NPRP@RGO anodes for SIBs is attributed to the structure design, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>, which illustrates the sodiation schematics of NPRP@RGO as anodes for SIBs. During the sodiation process (P + 3Na<sup>+</sup> + 3e<sup>–</sup> → Na<sub>3</sub>P), the NPRP is confined on RGO sheets by P–C chemical bonding, increasing the interface contact between the NPRP and the conductive network. The nanoporous structure of NPRP could accommodate RP volume expansion during the sodiation process, increase the contact area with the electrolyte, and shorten the ion transport distance. Additionally, the highly conductive network based on RGO can ensure the efficient and rapid electron and ion transport, which is illustrated by electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS). As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075/suppl_file/nn8b04075_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S14</a>, it is composed of a single depressed semicircle in the high–medium frequency region (corresponding to composite impedance of sodium-ion transport through the film surface and charge transfer at the electrolyte/electrode interface) and an inclined line (related to the Na-ion diffusion inside electrodes) at low frequency. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9 ref21 ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref21 ref33" aria-label="reference 9, 21 and 33">(9,21,33)</a> The charge transfer resistance of NPRP@RGO electrodes after 50 cycles is lower than those of commercial RP electrodes, indicating a rapid and efficient electron transport network of the NPRP@RGO electrodes. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075/suppl_file/nn8b04075_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S15</a> presents TEM images of NPRP@RGO anodes after 1500 cycles at 5.12 A g<sub>RP</sub><sup>–1</sup>. The NPRP does not pulverize completely, implying the marvelous structural stability of NPRP@RGO electrodes.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075/asset/images/medium/nn-2018-04075r_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075/asset/images/large/nn-2018-04075r_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Sodiation schematics of NPRP@RGO as anode for SIBs. NPRP@RGO can not only accommodate the volume changes efficiently but also ensure the rapid electron transport and ion diffusion.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075/asset/images/large/nn-2018-04075r_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.8b04075&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i6">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, we have demonstrated a scalable synthesis route for NPRP@RGO by boiling. Moreover, NPRP@RGO as anode for SIBs exhibits a superhigh capacity (1249.7 mAh g<sub>composite</sub><sup>–1</sup> after 150 cycles at 173.26 mA g<sub>composite</sub><sup>–1</sup>), superior rate capability (656.9 mAh g<sub>composite</sub><sup>–1</sup> at 3465.28 mA g<sub>composite</sub><sup>–1</sup>), and ultralong cycle life at 5.12 A g<sub>RP</sub><sup>–1</sup> for RP-based electrodes (775.3 mAh g<sub>RP</sub><sup>–1</sup> after 1500 cycles). The excellent electrochemical performance can be attributed to volume accommodation by the nanoporous structure and high electronic conductive network built on RGO sheets. The successful synthesis of NPRP@RGO provides a synthesis method for nanoporous structures, paving the way for enhancing the performance of SIBs and other electrochemical energy storage systems.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec4_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8"> Materials Synthesis</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Phosphorus triiodide (99%, Sigma-Aldrich), CTAB (99%, Aladdin), anhydrous ethylene glycol (99%, Aladdin), ethanol (99.7%, Sinopharm), iodobenzene (98%, Alfa Aesar), commercial red phosphorus powder (98.5%, Aladdin), graphene oxide (XFNANO), and hydrazine hydrate (N<sub>2</sub>H<sub>4</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O) (80%, Aladdin) were used in sample preparation. The NPRP@RGO was prepared with a typical redox reaction. Beforehand, a solution of PI<sub>3</sub> in iodobenzene (1.6 M) and a solution of additive (GO (10 mg/mL)) and CTAB (0.018 M) in ethylene glycol should be prepared. The PI<sub>3</sub> iodobenzene solution was injected into the GO and CTAB ethylene glycol solution with intense magnetic stirring for 10 min. After the reaction, N<sub>2</sub>H<sub>4</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O was added to the solution (mass ratio, N<sub>2</sub>H<sub>4</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O:GO = 2:1) in a water bath at 95 °C for 0.5 h. After 4000 rpm centrifugation, the precipitate was heated in a vacuum drying chamber at 200 °C for 6 h.</div></div><div id="sec4_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9"> Microstructural Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The microstructure of the prepared sample was characterized by scanning emission microscope (SEM, Hitachi SU-70) and high-resolution transmission electron microscope (JEOL JEM-2100). The structure and composition were characterized using XRD (Rigaku Dmax-rc diffractometer), a LabRAM HR800 spectrometer for Raman spectra, and an ESCALAB 250 for XPS (ThermoFisher Scientific). TGA was measured from 30 to 600 °C at a rate of 10 °C/min (Netzsch, STA 449F3-QMS403C). BET surface area and pore distribution plots were measured by a Micromeritics ASAP 2020.</div></div><div id="sec4_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i10"> Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The as-prepared active material was mixed with Super P (from lzy Battery Sales Department, China) and a carboxymethyl cellulose binder (Aladdin) (70:15:15 in weight) in deionized water to form a homogeneous slurry. Then it was painted on a copper foil (lzy Battery Sales Department, China) and then dried at 80 °C in a vacuum drying chamber for 10 h to form the electrodes. The areal loading mass of the active materials (RP) is about 0.3–0.4 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. A Na sheet (homemade) was used as the counter electrode, while Celgard 2400 was used as the separator. A mixture of 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in propylene carbonate with 5% fluoroethylene carbonate additive was used as the electrolyte. All the cells (CR2016 coin-type) (lzy Battery Sales Department, China) were assembled in a glovebox with an oxygen/water content lower than 1 ppm and tested at room temperature. CV measurements were carried out with coin cells at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> between 0.01 and 2.0 V (<i>vs</i> Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) using a CHI 660E electrochemical workstation (Shanghai, China). Galvanostatic discharge/charge cycles were conducted between 0.01 and 2.0 V (<i>vs</i> Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) on a Neware-CT-3008 test system (Shenzhen, China). EIS was also performed on a CHI 660E electrochemical workstation with a frequency of 100 kHz to 0.01 Hz.</div></div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i11">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsnano.8b04075</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Photographs of the synthesis process for NPRP@RGO; TEM images of NPRP@RGO at different synthesis stages and RP@RGO without boiling; SEM images of GO and RGO used in this study; BET analysis and TGA of NPRP@RGO; cyclic performance of NPRP@RGO, RP@RGO, and RGO as anodes for SIBs; Nyquist plots of NPRP@RGO and commercial RP electrodes; TEM images of NPRP@RGO electrodes after 1500 cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075/suppl_file/nn8b04075_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b04075/suppl_file/nn8b04075_si_001.pdf">nn8b04075_si_001.pdf (3.34 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsnano.8b04075" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Orderly Packed Anodes for High-Power Lithium-Ion Batteries with Super-Long Cycle Life: Rational Design of MnCO3/Large-Area Graphene Composites</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhong, Yiren; Yang, Mei; Zhou, Xianlong; Luo, Yuting; Wei, Jinping; Zhou, Zhen</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Large-area MnCO3/graphene composites were achieved by means of a reactant-conversion route starting from large-area graphene (LG) and MnOOH precursor.  This delicately designed architecture with self-assembly into orderly packed electrodes offers several advantages as lithium-ion battery (LIB) anodes.  First, LG offers perfect large-area support for MnCO3 particles with uniform dispersion, allowing structural re-organization to be accommodated by the space between particles during cycling.  Second, the continuous graphene networks existing in an ordered macroscopic electrode guarantee outstanding ion diffusion and electronic conduction, readily yielding high power delivery.  Third, the inter-plane compact configuration of graphene domains provides sustained structural stability and high-packing-d. energy storage.  Profiting from the combination of uninterrupted ion transport and effective structural stabilization, the MnCO3-LG electrode delivered excellent electrochem. performance, and represents state-of-the-art design of high-power, high-efficiency, and low-cost anodes for next-generation LIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp-beLZ46MP1rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljcLLiWqlB6vA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXitFaiurnM&amp;md5=62b06a4f9d64e5f3ad886ca9009eadc1</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1021/nl502759z"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Song, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gordin, M.                         cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Chemically Bonded Phosphorus/Graphene Hybrid as a High Performance Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Song, Jiangxuan; Yu, Zhaoxin; Gordin, Mikhail L.; Hu, Shi; Yi, Ran; Tang, Duihai; Walter, Timothy; Regula, Michael; Choi, Daiwon; Li, Xiaolin; Manivannan, Ayyakkannu; Wang, Donghai</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
